The massive cluster strong-lens scale is typically \u03b8<SUB>E</SUB> \u223c 10\u2033 to \u223c30\u2033-35\u2033, with only a handful of clusters known with Einstein radii \u03b8<SUB>E</SUB> \u223c 40\u2033 or above (for z<SUB>source</SUB> = 2, nominally). Here we report another very large cluster lens, RXC J0032.1+1808 (z = 0.3956), the second-richest cluster in the redMapper cluster catalog and the 85th most massive cluster in the Planck Sunyaev-Zel'dovich catalog. With our light-traces-mass and fully parametric approaches, we construct strong-lensing models based on 18 multiple images of five background galaxies newly identified in the Hubble data, mainly from the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS), in addition to a known sextuply imaged system in this cluster. Furthermore, we compare these models to Lenstool and GLAFIC models that were produced independently as part of the RELICS program. All models reveal a large effective Einstein radius of \u03b8<SUB>E</SUB> \u2243 40\u2033 (z<SUB>source</SUB> = 2), owing to the obvious concentration of substructures near the cluster center. Although RXC J0032.1+1808 has a very large critical area and high lensing strength, only three magnified high-redshift candidates are found within the field targeted by RELICS. Nevertheless, we expect many more high-redshift candidates will be seen in wider and deeper observations with Hubble or the James Webb Space Telescope. Finally, the comparison between several algorithms demonstrates that the total error budget is largely dominated by systematic uncertainties.", "doi": "10.3847/1538-4357/ab929d", "custom1": "eprint: <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F898%2F1%2F6\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/898/1/6</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <p>Extensive surveys with the Hubble Space Telescope over the past decade, targeting some of the most massive clusters in the sky, have uncovered dozens of galaxy cluster strong lenses. The massive cluster strong-lens scale is typically <i>\u03b8</i><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a010'' to \u223c30''\u201335'', with only a handful of clusters known with Einstein radii <i>\u03b8</i><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a040'' or above (for <i>z</i><sub>source</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02, nominally). Here we report another very large cluster lens, RXC J0032.1+1808 (<i>z</i>\u00a0=\u00a00.3956), the second-richest cluster in the redMapper cluster catalog and the 85th most massive cluster in the Planck Sunyaev\u2013Zel'dovich catalog. With our light-traces-mass and fully parametric approaches, we construct strong-lensing models based on 18 multiple images of five background galaxies newly identified in the Hubble data, mainly from the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS), in addition to a known sextuply imaged system in this cluster. Furthermore, we compare these models to Lenstool and GLAFIC models that were produced independently as part of the RELICS program. All models reveal a large effective Einstein radius of <i>\u03b8</i><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a040'' (<i>z</i><sub>source</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02), owing to the obvious concentration of substructures near the cluster center. Although RXC J0032.1+1808 has a very large critical area and high lensing strength, only three magnified high-redshift candidates are found within the field targeted by RELICS. Nevertheless, we expect many more high-redshift candidates will be seen in wider and deeper observations with Hubble or the James Webb Space Telescope. Finally, the comparison between several algorithms demonstrates that the total error budget is largely dominated by systematic uncertainties.</p></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export class=\"linked-articles__btn event_read-more-articles\" data-event-action=\"Next Article\"><b>Next</b> article in issue<span\n\t\t\t\t\tclass=\"fa-icon fa-icon--right\"><svg aria-hidden=\"true\" focusable=\"false\"\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\txmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/2000/svg\" viewBox=\"0 0 192 512\"><path\n\t\t\t\t\td=\"M0 384.662V127.338c0-17.818 21.543-26.741 34.142-14.142l128.662 class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2020ApJ...898....6A\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2020ApJ...898....6A&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2020ApJ...898....6A\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds1\" name=\"apjab929ds1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Over the past decade, extensive galaxy cluster lensing campaigns have been undertaken with the Hubble Space Telescope (HST; Postman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib53\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib53\">2012</a>; Schmidt et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib62\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib62\">2014</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib70\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib70\">2015</a>; Lotz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib38\">2017</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>; Steinhardt et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib66\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib66\">2020</a>). HST's unique combination of sensitivity and resolution allows for the identification of lensed galaxies that are multiply imaged by the targeted galaxy clusters (see, for instance, Franx et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib25\">1997</a>; Frye &amp; Broadhurst <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib26\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib26\">1998</a>; Broadhurst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib10\">2005</a>; Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib20\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib20\">2018</a>; Caminha et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib12\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib12\">2019</a>; Jauzac et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib31\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib31\">2019</a>; Lagattuta et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib37\">2019</a>). These multiple images, in turn, allow us to construct mass models for the clusters, describing the underlying matter distribution. Most (albeit not all) of the clusters targeted with HST, which are typically estimated to be massive based on X-rays, the Sunyaev\u2013Zeldovich effect (SZ; Sunyaev &amp; Zeldovich <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib67\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib67\">1970</a>), or optical-richness criteria (and lensing signatures such as giant arcs in ground-based data), show multiply imaged background galaxies in numbers that generally increase with the strong-lens scale (or critical area, i.e., the area enclosed by the critical curves of infinite magnification; see, for instance, Vega-Ferrero et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib72\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib72\">2019</a>).</p><p>Following the high projected-mass densities in their centers, the strong-lens scale of galaxy clusters is expected to reach <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> of the order of tens of arcseconds, with <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> being the effective Einstein radius (i.e., the radius of the area enclosed by the critical curves of the lens, were it a circle). This is indeed the range of typical Einstein radii found in lensing analyses of well-known clusters (Richard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib58\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib58\">2010b</a>; Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib49\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib49\">2012</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>; Sharon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib63\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib63\">2020</a>). Since the Einstein radius size essentially depends on the mass enclosed in the core of the cluster, the distribution of Einstein radii can be used to probe cosmological models as well as structure formation and evolution in its framework (Turner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib71\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib71\">1984</a>; Narayan &amp; White <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib45\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib45\">1988</a>; Oguri &amp; Blandford <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib50\">2009</a>). Due to the shape of the cosmic mass function (Tinker et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib68\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib68\">2008</a>), more massive clusters and thus, generally, larger Einstein radii become rarer (see, for instance, Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib49\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib49\">2012</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib75\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib75\">2012</a>). Other effects are also known to boost the lensing cross section such as a major axis oriented along the line of sight, a high degree of substructure, cluster concentration, or the dynamical state (Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib42\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib42\">2010</a>). Indeed, only a handful of clusters are known to have Einstein radii of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u2273\u00a035'', for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a02, nominally (see the list in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt1\">1</a>). The number of clusters with particularly large Einstein radii is therefore very important because it can place useful constraints on structure formation and evolution models (e.g., Oguri &amp; Blandford <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib50\">2009</a>).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjab929dt1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nKnown Strong-lensing Clusters with Einstein Radii above <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a035'' (<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02)<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dt1fna\">a</a></sup>\n\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib57\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib57\">2010a</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">RXC J0032.1+1808</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">00:32:11</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">+18:07:49</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">0.3956</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u223c40''</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">RELICS</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">This work</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">PLCK G171.9-40.7</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">03:12:57</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">+08:22:19</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">0.2700</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u223c37''</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">RELICS</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Acebron et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib64\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib64\">2015</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Notes.</strong></p>\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fna\" id=\"apjab929dt1fna\"></a><sup> a</sup>We note that there are other clusters that were claimed to have large Einstein radii, but later downsized in updated analyses. For example, both MACS J2129.4\u20130741 and MACS J0257.1-2325 were reported by Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib74\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib74\">2011</a>) to have Einstein radii above 35'', yet an updated analysis with additional broadband data (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>; A. Zitrin 2020, in preparation) suggests these are smaller lenses. As another example, RX J1347-1145 was analyzed by Halkola et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib29\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib29\">2008</a>) to have an Einstein radius above 35'' as well. An updated analysis using CLASH data by Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>), resulted in a somewhat smaller value of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a033'' for a redshift <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.0.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnb\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnb\"></a><sup> b</sup>The Einstein radii are obtained from strong-lensing analyses using different algorithms.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnc\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnc\"></a><sup> c</sup>Recent HST lensing surveys that included the cluster.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnd\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnd\"></a><sup> d</sup>First strong-lensing analysis to publish the size of the lens. More references are available in the literature for some of the clusters.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fne\" id=\"apjab929dt1fne\"></a><sup> e</sup>Hubble Frontier Fields Survey; see Lotz et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib38\">2017</a>).\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnf\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnf\"></a><sup> f</sup>Cluster Lensing And Supernova Survey with Hubble; see Postman et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib53\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib53\">2012</a>).\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fng\" id=\"apjab929dt1fng\"></a><sup> g</sup>Beyond Ultra-deep Frontier Fields and Legacy Observations Survey; see Steinhardt et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib66\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib66\">2020</a>).\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnh\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnh\"></a><sup> h</sup>Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>).\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/898/1/6/suppdata/apjab929dt1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/ab929d\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dt1_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=S62EzP6mbyMm9IDIEcxZDG3qLmk%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>Larger strong lenses (presenting larger Einstein radii) should have on average larger areas of high magnification and thus lens more background sources (Vega-Ferrero et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib72\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib72\">2019</a>), especially if the faint-end slope of the luminosity function is steep enough (Broadhurst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib11\">1995</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib9\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib9\">2014</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib16\">2015</a>), as is currently estimated, for example, for high-redshift galaxies (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib8\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib8\">2015</a>; Finkelstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib24\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib24\">2015</a>; Mason et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib40\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib40\">2015</a>). Building a census of this particular class of large cluster lenses is thus also important for efficiently searching for high-redshift galaxies with current and future observations.</p><p>The Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS; PI: Coe; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>) is a large Hubble Space Telescope program that has observed 41 galaxy clusters chosen largely based on SZ-mass estimates from the Planck PSZ2 all-sky catalog (Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib52\">2016</a>). One of the chief goals of the RELICS survey is to identify bright high-redshift galaxies that could be followed up from the ground and with the upcoming James Webb Space Telescope. Lens models for the observed clusters are needed to study the dark matter (DM) distribution, as well as the intrinsic properties of newly uncovered high-redshift galaxy candidates lensed by these clusters (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib60\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib60\">2018</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib61\">2020</a>).</p><p>In our systematic analysis of RELICS clusters (Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib2\">2018</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib1\">2019</a>; Cerny et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib13\">2018</a>; Cibirka et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib14\">2018</a>; Paterno-Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib51\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib51\">2018</a>; Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib39\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib39\">2019</a>), we have analyzed RXC J0032.1+1808<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn1\">25</a></sup>\n (B\u00f6hringer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib7\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib7\">2001</a>; Ebeling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib21\">2001</a>), located at R.A.\u00a0=\u00a000<sup>h</sup>32<sup>m</sup>11<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fs.gif\" alt=\"fs\" align=\"baseline\" />0, decl.\u00a0=\u00a0+18<sup>d</sup>07<sup>m</sup>49<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fs.gif\" alt=\"fs\" align=\"baseline\" />0 at a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.3956. RXC J0032.1+1808 (RXC0032 hereafter) is the second-richest galaxy cluster in the Sloan Digital Sky Survey DR8 redMaPPer cluster catalog (only after RMJ224319.8-093530.9; see Rykoff et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib59\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib59\">2014</a>), but only the 85th most massive cluster in the PSZ2 catalog (Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib52\">2016</a>), with a mass of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn1.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=tjni5SozzfYdWEo2Wv2gy8D5EMo%3D\" alt=\"${M}_{500}={7.61}_{-0.63}^{+0.57}\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{500}={7.61}_{-0.63}^{+0.57}\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>, where <em>M</em><sub>500</sub> is defined as the cluster mass within the radius <em>R</em><sub>500</sub> inside which the mean mass density is 500 times the critical density <em>\u03b4</em><sub><em>c</em></sub>.</p><p>A first strong-lensing (SL) model for this cluster, prior to RELICS imaging, was used in Dessauges-Zavadsky et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib19\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib19\">2017</a>) to estimate the properties of a notable multiply imaged system at a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.6314 (system 1 and 2 here). Here we present our SL analysis of RXC0032 in RELICS data, revealing a very large critical area, similar to only a handful of other clusters known to date (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt1\">1</a>). We report here this discovery and detail our SL modeling.</p><p>The outline of the paper is as follows: in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds2\">2</a> we briefly describe the data and observations used to identify multiple images for the SL analysis, which is presented in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds3\">3</a>. The results are shown and discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds4\">4</a>. We compare our results with those obtained from the Lenstool and GLAFIC pipelines<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn2\">26</a></sup>\n in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds5\">5</a>. Finally, we discuss the previously detected lensed star-forming galaxy in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds6\">6</a> before summarizing our work in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds7\">7</a>. Throughout we assume a \u039bCDM cosmology with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn2.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=4%2FaCaMUN1zsxjKFRpfphGXqdO3w%3D\" alt=\"${{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{m}}0}=0.3$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{m}}0}=0.3</script></span></span>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn3.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=TFcDHoh%2FkgB%2FfafFVWHfSVGAcZA%3D\" alt=\"${{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{\\Lambda }}0}=0.7$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{\\Lambda }}0}=0.7</script></span></span>, <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0= 70 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> where 1''\u00a0=\u00a05.337 kpc at the redshift of RXC J0032.1+1808.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds2\" name=\"apjab929ds2\">2.\u00a0Data and Observations</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>RXC0032 is part of the RELICS cluster sample (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>). Each cluster field in the RELICS program was observed for two orbits with Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3/IR) in the F105W, F125W, F140W, and F160W bands, and complemented archival observations with the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS) so that each field is observed for three orbits\u2014one in each of the passbands F435W, F606W, and F814W. In addition, 30 hr per band in each of the Spitzer-IRAC channels (PI: M. Bradac, PI: Soifer) were observed. As one orbit of HST archival observations already existed for RXC0032 (program GO 12166, PI: Ebeling), RELICS observed this galaxy cluster for only two orbits with each of the ACS bands, in addition to the two WFC3 orbits (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>).</p><p>Data reduction of the HST images is described in Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>). We used the photometric source catalogs generated with Source Extractor (Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib6\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib6\">1996</a>) in dual-image mode from the final drizzled 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />06 pixel<sup>\u22121</sup> images. The photometric redshifts we use (hereafter <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>), were derived using the Bayesian Photometric Redshift program (BPZ, Ben\u00edtez <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib3\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib3\">2000</a>; Ben\u00edtez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib4\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib4\">2004</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib15\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib15\">2006</a>) from seven HST band imaging data (from the combined RELICS and the aforementioned archival HST data). The reduced imaging, catalogs, and data products are available for the community through the Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes (MAST).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn3\">27</a></sup>\n\n</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds3\" name=\"apjab929ds3\">3.\u00a0Strong-lensing Formalism</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We construct lens models for RXC0032 using two methodologies. Primarily, we use the light-traces-mass (LTM) methodology, outlined in Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>) and references therein; see also Broadhurst et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib10\">2005</a>). With this methodology, we uncover multiple-image sets and publish the first estimate for the size of the lens. We also use a fully parametric formalism for comparison, dubbed hereafter dPIEeNFW. Both methodologies assume two main mass components for the mass distribution: one that accounts for the cluster galaxies, and a second that represents the DM distribution. While member galaxies are also represented differently, the main difference between the two methodologies is that in the LTM methodology the DM distribution is assumed to follow the light distribution, whereas in the parametric model it is independent and follows a combination of symmetric, analytic forms. Both methodologies are implemented on a grid where the resolution can be changed for computational time purposes. Both methodologies are implemented in the same pipeline (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>), and are briefly detailed below.</p><h3 id=\"apjab929ds3-1\" name=\"apjab929ds3-1\">3.1.\u00a0LTM</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The LTM formalism is based on the assumption that the cluster member luminosity-weighted distribution is a reasonable tracer for the DM component in the cluster. The first ingredient for the model are the cluster galaxies, in which each galaxy is assigned a power-law surface density mass distribution, scaled by its luminosity. The exponent is the same for all galaxies and the superposition of all galaxy contributions constitutes the member-galaxies component of the model. This map is then smoothed with a Gaussian kernel to represent the DM distribution component. The two components are then added with a relative weight and scaled to match a multiple-image system (or redshift) of choice. This basic model includes only four free parameters: the power-law exponent for the mass distribution of the cluster galaxies, the width of the smoothing Gaussian, the galaxy to DM weight, and the overall normalization. In addition, we typically include a two-parameter external shear to add further flexibility (manifested mainly in the form of ellipticity of the critical curves), bringing the number of free parameters to six. It is also possible to leave some galaxy masses to be independently scaled\u2014especially for brighter cluster galaxies, for which we typically find the mass to light (<em>M/L</em>) ratio to be a few times higher than that of other cluster members. These key galaxies can also be assigned an ellipticity, and a core. Finally, the redshifts of systems with no spectroscopic redshift can be optimized by the lens model.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab929ds3-2\" name=\"apjab929ds3-2\">3.2.\u00a0dPIEeNFW</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our parametric formalism is based on analytic functions for both the member galaxies and the DM components. Galaxy-scale halos are parameterized each as a double pseudoisothermal elliptical (dPIE) mass distribution<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn4\">28</a></sup>\n (see, for instance, El\u00edasd\u00f3ttir et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib22\">2007</a>). Their velocity dispersion, core radius and cutoff radius are scaled based on their luminosity following common scaling relations. These are found to describe early-type galaxies well and can be defined with respect to a typical reference luminosity of a galaxy at the cluster's redshift (Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib33\">2007</a>; Monna et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib44\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib44\">2015</a>; Bergamini et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib5\">2019</a>). All cluster galaxies, aside from the few brightest cluster galaxies in the cluster core, have no ellipticities assigned to them and their positional parameters are fixed to those derived from their light distribution. Each large-scale DM halo is represented by an elliptical Navarro, Frenk, and White mass distribution (eNFW, Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib46\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib46\">1996</a>) where their concentration, mass, ellipticity, and position angle are free parameters of the model. The central positions of the eNFW halos can also be freely optimized but here they are fixed to the light centroid of the brightest cluster galaxies.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab929ds3-3\" name=\"apjab929ds3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Minimization</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>For both methodologies, the best-fitting model parameters are found by minimizing the distance in the image plane between the observed and model-predicted positions of the multiple-image centers, via a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> criterion (the equations for the <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> and rms calculations are presented in Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib1\">2019</a>). To do so, we use a Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) engine with a Metropolis\u2013Hastings algorithm that typically includes several thousand steps after the burn-in phase, from which both the best-fit model and the uncertainties are derived. For more details on the modeling scheme, see Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>) and references therein.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab929ds3-4\" name=\"apjab929ds3-4\">3.4.\u00a0SL Analysis of RXC J0032.1+1808</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The starting point of our modeling relies on the construction of a cluster member catalog based on the red-sequence method (Gladders &amp; Yee <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib28\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib28\">2000</a>). We use the magnitudes measured from the F606W and F814W filters to draw a color\u2013magnitude diagram. We only consider galaxies down to 24 AB within \u00b10.3 mag of the sequence (De Lucia &amp; Helmi <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib18\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib18\">2008</a>). To exclude stars from our selection we do not include objects whose magnitudes are brighter than 17 AB or have a stellarity index below &lt;0.95. In addition, we take advantage of the delivered photometric catalog by RELICS to check that all selected cluster members were within <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 of the mean redshift of the cluster (as measured by the BPZ software). We finally perform a visual inspection of the selected cluster members. This allows us to discard further interloping galaxies (bright foreground galaxies for instance) or artifacts (such as faint and diffuse objects or double detections), or add missing galaxies that appear to be cluster members based on their colors (not selected initially due to the strict magnitude cuts applied).</p><p>For the minimization, we consider (for both models) a positional uncertainty of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4. This value has been found to encompass both the underlying statistical uncertainties, the systematic uncertainties between our LTM and dPIEeNFW methods, as well as possible uncertainties arising from structure along the line of sight (Host <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib30\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib30\">2012</a>). To speed up the runtime of the models, and while RELICS HST images have a resolution of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />06 pixel<sup>\u22121</sup>, we adopt a resolution of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />24 pixel<sup>\u22121</sup> for the minimization.</p><p>We present now the multiply imaged systems used in this work, labeled in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a> and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>. We first include the multiply imaged system reported by Dessauges-Zavadsky et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib19\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib19\">2017</a>) who identified six two-knot images belonging to this lensed object. We label this as systems 1 and 2 in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a> and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>, corresponding to the two different emission knots.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab929df1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929df1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=uUf3DfNyE7jJTYiezrll4P%2B6wcY%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=uUf3DfNyE7jJTYiezrll4P%2B6wcY%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Color-composite RGB image of RXC0032. The image was constructed with the HST/ACS passbands F435W (blue), a combination of F606W+F814W (green), and a combination of the HST/WFC3IR passbands F105W+F125W+F140W+F160W (red). The resulting critical curves from our best-fit LTM model are displayed for a source at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.0 (in green) and <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a09.0 (in violet). In addition, we add the critical lines at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a09.0 from our fully parametric best-fit dPIEeNFW model (in red). Multiple images (color coded to ease their identification) are numbered according to Table <a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a>. Cyan systems are reported as candidates and not used in the LTM/dPIEeNFW models. Systems reported by the Lenstool and/or GLAFIC modeling teams are indicated in light gray. Labeled galaxies indicate the cluster members whose weight is freely optimized in the LTM model. The positions of the large-scale dark matter halos for the dPIEeNFW model are fixed to the position of the galaxies A, B, and C. The ellipticities and position angles of the two cluster galaxies, B and C, are freely optimized in both models. Finally, we choose galaxy A as the center for the computation of the convergence profile in Figure <a href=\"#apjab929df2\">2</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab929df1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=uUf3DfNyE7jJTYiezrll4P%2B6wcY%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab929df1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df1_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=O3qWRYwnVrHLeQDmRimlwn0Uo%2BU%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>In addition, based on the full HST ACS + WFC3/IR data set, now including RELICS imaging, we detect additional multiply imaged systems (without a spectroscopic confirmation yet), which we identify based on their morphology and color similarity, as well as predictions from a preliminary LTM model. These images are included as lensing constraints in our final models and we briefly review them here. Systems 3, 4, and c5 straddle the critical curve each forming two bright knots stretching into an arc with their counterimages on the opposite side of the cluster (we note, however, that the identification of the counterimage remains less secure; the LTM, dPIEeNFW, and GLAFIC models implement this image in the SL modeling while the Lenstool model does not). System 6 is lensed into three images appearing as two distinct pink and blue knots (systems 6.1 and 6.2 in our modeling) in the composite HST ACS and WCF3/IR images (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>), making this identification reliable. System 7 is lensed into three images which are identified mainly thanks to their similar, red dropout colors as they do not present any peculiar morphology. This system is a relatively high-redshift dropout object (<em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a04.41 based on its first image for which the photometric redshift is the most reliable), hence its redshift is assumed correct and we fix it in our model. Finally, system 9 is lensed into three diffuse red images (in the ACS and WFC3 composite image). We also identify additional, potential systems that were, however, not included in our SL modeling as constraints. System c8 appears to be lensed into two images straddling the critical curve. We do not, however, identify a third counterimage on the other side of the critical curves so we report it only as a candidate system. System c10 lies next to system 9 and is comprised of two faint emission knots. Our SL models predict additional counterimages near a group of cluster member galaxies located at R.A.\u00a0=\u00a08.0470889 deg; decl.\u00a0= 18.117521 deg. However, due to the light contamination, we cannot make a reliable identification. Systems c11, c12, and c13 consist each of two arcs straddling the critical curve in the northern region of the cluster. Lacking a WFC3/IR coverage in that northern area of the cluster, and BPZ yielding significantly different photometric redshift estimates, we decided to keep these systems as candidate systems. Spectroscopic confirmation of these systems would help to more accurately constrain the mass distribution in the most northern region, where no other lensing constraints are seen.</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><em>LTM</em>. The LTM model is built by considering the weight of the five brightest cluster members (labeled in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>) as free parameters, i.e., allowing their <em>M/L</em> ratio to vary. We also consider as free parameters the ellipticity (varying within a flat, small prior of \u00b10.05 from the measured value from the light distribution) and position angle (varying within \u00b15\u00b0 from the input value we measured) of the bright galaxies located at R.A.\u00a0=\u00a08.04691, decl.\u00a0=\u00a018.118922; and R.A.\u00a0=\u00a08.039185, decl.\u00a0=\u00a018.115616, (galaxies B and C in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>).We scale our model to the spectroscopic redshift of systems 1 and 2 (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a>). The redshift of the remaining systems, except dropout system 7, are left as free parameters to be optimized in the minimization procedure (allowing the relative <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn4.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=cSEMeJMYdmPi4HBg5MBJmGu%2BvTg%3D\" alt=\"${D}_{\\mathrm{LS}}/{D}_{{\\rm{S}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{D}_{\\mathrm{LS}}/{D}_{{\\rm{S}}}</script></span></span> ratio for each system, corresponding to its best-fit <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub> value, to vary by up to \u00b10.05; with <em>D</em><sub>S</sub> and <em>D</em><sub>LS</sub> being the angular diameter distances to the source and between the lens and the source). Taking into account the additional freely optimized cluster members and source redshifts, our final model includes a total of 20 free parameters. The resulting critical curves (for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02 and <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09) for our final best-fit model which has an image reproduction rms\u00a0=\u00a01<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />60, are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>. Finally, the image reproductions by our LTM lens model are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df6\">B1</a>, showing that the overall shape, orientation, and internal details are satisfactorily recovered.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><em>dPIEeNFW</em>. In merging clusters such as the one analyzed here, multiple DM halos are usually incorporated in the modeling in order to better explain the mass distribution. Our best-fit model comprises three large-scale DM halos whose centers are fixed to the position of the cluster members labeled A, B, and C in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>. The large-scale DM halos are parameterized with eNFWs, where their ellipticity parameters, concentration, and mass are optimized. Cluster members are modeled with a dPIE profile with a fixed core radius of 0.2 kpc for the reference galaxy, a velocity dispersion that is allowed to vary between 80\u2013120 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, and a cutoff radius varying from 45\u201365 kpc. They are modeled as spherical with a mag0\u00a0=\u00a0\u221221.56 (a reference magnitude for the scaling relations; Faber &amp; Jackson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib23\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib23\">1976</a>; Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib33\">2007</a>). Similarly as in the LTM model, we leave the ellipticities and position angles of the two bright galaxies presented above to be optimized.We adopt the same multiple images and cluster member catalogs as for the LTM model, leaving as free parameters the redshifts of background sources that have not been spectroscopically confirmed (except for system 7). Our final model includes a total of 24 free parameters. Our best-fit model has an image reproduction of rms\u00a0=\u00a01<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />43 and for comparison, we also show in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a> the resulting critical curves for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds4\" name=\"apjab929ds4\">4.\u00a0Results and Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a> shows the critical curves from our lens models. Both the LTM and dPIEeNFW models, despite having a very different representation for the different mass components, overall yield similar critical curves. This is perhaps somewhat expected, given that similar sets of multiple images were used as constraints, although notable differences exist as well\u2014especially in regions of high magnification or regions with fewer constraints.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df2\">2</a> shows the convergence profile for RXC0032. The SL region is dominated by a large number of substructures, accounting for the shallow inner profile. We also note that the resulting mass distribution has a high overall elongation, or ellipticity, computed as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn5.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=oBrNEJxgXFhaujXWdn7eYnTaubM%3D\" alt=\"$e=({a}^{2}-{b}^{2})/({a}^{2}+{b}^{2})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">e=({a}^{2}-{b}^{2})/({a}^{2}+{b}^{2})</script></span></span>, of <em>e</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a00.74(0.81)\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03 from the LTM(dPIEeNFW) models.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab929df2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929df2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=xpg0L3a0omBZBWPmNvlkS4rMI7A%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Convergence profiles from our best-fit LTM and dPIEeNFW models, scaled to a source redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.0. The vertical dashed line indicates the radius within which multiple images have been identified.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab929df2\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=xpg0L3a0omBZBWPmNvlkS4rMI7A%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab929df2\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df2_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=B038cH3w%2BX8PVLHBaG0UJgGlBWg%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We compute the value of the effective Einstein radius as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn6.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=LWUuofsHCdapqMX0ZbqBrt9Dl%2B0%3D\" alt=\"${\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}=\\sqrt{A/\\pi }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}=\\sqrt{A/\\pi }</script></span></span>, with <em>A</em> defined as the area enclosed within the critical curves. Our SL analysis reveals a particularly prominent lens with a resulting Einstein radii of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" 0\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 20</script></span></span> and <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>(<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09)\u00a0\u223c\u00a048<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />10(48<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />10)\u00a0\u00b1 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />25 from our LTM(dPIEeNFW) best-fit models, corresponding to an enclosed mass of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn8.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=LGajJ0jqTUJ8IsCmsm32svrLxNU%3D\" alt=\"$M(\\lt {\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}})=2.13(2.67)\\pm 0.2\\,\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">M(\\lt {\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}})=2.13(2.67)\\pm 0.2\\,\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span> within the <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02 critical curves. The high degree of substructures aggregated in the center yields this very large Einstein radius, similar to only a few other clusters. While the SZ signal, and gas probes in general, lead to the discovery of clusters of high virial mass (Williamson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib73\">2011</a>; Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib52\">2016</a>), a high total mass does not guarantee a large SL region, and in the case of RXC0032, it seems that the high cluster richness, for example, better traces the large Einstein radius. RXC0032 portrays the loose relation\u2014or at least the large scatter in the relation\u2014between gas probes (and the SZ effect in particular) and the central SL area, which depends more closely on various other factors (Giocoli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib27\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib27\">2016</a>) and especially the amount of matter concentrated or projected in the very center, the cluster triaxial shape, and its orientation along the line of sight (King &amp; Corless <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib36\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib36\">2007</a>; Oguri &amp; Blandford <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib50\">2009</a>).</p><p>We show in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df3\">3</a> the <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09 magnification map from our LTM best-fit model as well as the location of the detected high-redshift galaxy candidates within RXC0032's field of view covered by both ACS and WFC3 (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib61\">2020</a>). We compare in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt2\">2</a> the magnification estimates from both methods and provide the intrinsic <em>M</em><sub>uv</sub> at <em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0=\u00a01500 \u00c5 for the high-redshift candidates.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab929df3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929df3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=%2BdunG7btjVEdszJJ22p%2BTj8V064%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=%2BdunG7btjVEdszJJ22p%2BTj8V064%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Left panel: magnification map from our best-fit LTM model for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a06 in which the RELICS high-<em>z</em> candidates found by Salmon et al. Right panel: Cumulative area having a magnification higher than a given value for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09.0 in RXC0032's field from our LTM and dPIEeNFW, Lenstool, and GLAFIC best-fit models. For comparison, we also show the lensing strengths from other RELICS SL clusters modeled with the LTM pipeline, MACS J0308.9+2645, PLCK G171.9-40.7 and Abell S295, A697, MACS J0025.4-1222, MACS J0159.8-0849, and RXJ0152.7-135. The cumulative areas (<em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05 and <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a010) for the Hubble Frontier Fields clusters are also indicated as colored stars, computed from the submitted <span class=\"small-caps\">zitrin-ltm-Gauss</span> models. The 1<em>\u03c3</em> errors are typically of the size of the star symbol. It should be noted that different clusters have been modeled with different fields of view. For example, the <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05 and <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a010 values presented for the HFF cluster MACSJ0717.5+3745 are likely to be underestimated since its critical curves
