(<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib2\">2018</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">RCS2 J232727.6-020437</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">23:27:08</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u221202:04:54</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">0.6986</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u223c35''</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">RELICS</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Sharon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib64\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib64\">2015</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Notes.</strong></p>\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fna\" id=\"apjab929dt1fna\"></a><sup> a</sup>We note that there are other clusters that were claimed to have large Einstein radii, but later downsized in updated analyses. For example, both MACS J2129.4\u20130741 and MACS J0257.1-2325 were reported by Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib74\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib74\">2011</a>) to have Einstein radii above 35'', yet an updated analysis with additional broadband data (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>; A. Zitrin 2020, in preparation) suggests these are smaller lenses. As another example, RX J1347-1145 was analyzed by Halkola et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib29\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib29\">2008</a>) to have an Einstein radius above 35'' as well. An updated analysis using CLASH data by Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>), resulted in a somewhat smaller value of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a033'' for a redshift <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.0.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnb\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnb\"></a><sup> b</sup>The Einstein radii are obtained from strong-lensing analyses using different algorithms.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnc\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnc\"></a><sup> c</sup>Recent HST lensing surveys that included the cluster.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnd\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnd\"></a><sup> d</sup>First strong-lensing analysis to publish the size of the lens. More references are available in the literature for some of the clusters.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fne\" id=\"apjab929dt1fne\"></a><sup> e</sup>Hubble Frontier Fields Survey; see Lotz et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib38\">2017</a>).\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnf\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnf\"></a><sup> f</sup>Cluster Lensing And Supernova Survey with Hubble; see Postman et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib53\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib53\">2012</a>).\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fng\" id=\"apjab929dt1fng\"></a><sup> g</sup>Beyond Ultra-deep Frontier Fields and Legacy Observations Survey; see Steinhardt et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib66\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib66\">2020</a>).\n<a name=\"apjab929dt1fnh\" id=\"apjab929dt1fnh\"></a><sup> h</sup>Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>).\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/898/1/6/suppdata/apjab929dt1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/ab929d\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dt1_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=S62EzP6mbyMm9IDIEcxZDG3qLmk%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>Larger strong lenses (presenting larger Einstein radii) should have on average larger areas of high magnification and thus lens more background sources (Vega-Ferrero et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib72\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib72\">2019</a>), especially if the faint-end slope of the luminosity function is steep enough (Broadhurst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib11\">1995</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib9\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib9\">2014</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib16\">2015</a>), as is currently estimated, for example, for high-redshift galaxies (Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib8\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib8\">2015</a>; Finkelstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib24\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib24\">2015</a>; Mason et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib40\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib40\">2015</a>). Building a census of this particular class of large cluster lenses is thus also important for efficiently searching for high-redshift galaxies with current and future observations.</p><p>The Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS; PI: Coe; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>) is a large Hubble Space Telescope program that has observed 41 galaxy clusters chosen largely based on SZ-mass estimates from the Planck PSZ2 all-sky catalog (Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib52\">2016</a>). One of the chief goals of the RELICS survey is to identify bright high-redshift galaxies that could be followed up from the ground and with the upcoming James Webb Space Telescope. Lens models for the observed clusters are needed to study the dark matter (DM) distribution, as well as the intrinsic properties of newly uncovered high-redshift galaxy candidates lensed by these clusters (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib60\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib60\">2018</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib61\">2020</a>).</p><p>In our systematic analysis of RELICS clusters (Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib2\">2018</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib1\">2019</a>; Cerny et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib13\">2018</a>; Cibirka et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib14\">2018</a>; Paterno-Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib51\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib51\">2018</a>; Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib39\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib39\">2019</a>), we have analyzed RXC J0032.1+1808<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn1\">25</a></sup>\n (B\u00f6hringer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib7\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib7\">2001</a>; Ebeling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib21\">2001</a>), located at R.A.\u00a0=\u00a000<sup>h</sup>32<sup>m</sup>11<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fs.gif\" alt=\"fs\" align=\"baseline\" />0, decl.\u00a0=\u00a0+18<sup>d</sup>07<sup>m</sup>49<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fs.gif\" alt=\"fs\" align=\"baseline\" />0 at a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.3956. RXC J0032.1+1808 (RXC0032 hereafter) is the second-richest galaxy cluster in the Sloan Digital Sky Survey DR8 redMaPPer cluster catalog (only after RMJ224319.8-093530.9; see Rykoff et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib59\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib59\">2014</a>), but only the 85th most massive cluster in the PSZ2 catalog (Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib52\">2016</a>), with a mass of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn1.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=tjni5SozzfYdWEo2Wv2gy8D5EMo%3D\" alt=\"${M}_{500}={7.61}_{-0.63}^{+0.57}\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{500}={7.61}_{-0.63}^{+0.57}\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>, where <em>M</em><sub>500</sub> is defined as the cluster mass within the radius <em>R</em><sub>500</sub> inside which the mean mass density is 500 times the critical density <em>\u03b4</em><sub><em>c</em></sub>.</p><p>A first strong-lensing (SL) model for this cluster, prior to RELICS imaging, was used in Dessauges-Zavadsky et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib19\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib19\">2017</a>) to estimate the properties of a notable multiply imaged system at a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.6314 (system 1 and 2 here). Here we present our SL analysis of RXC0032 in RELICS data, revealing a very large critical area, similar to only a handful of other clusters known to date (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt1\">1</a>). We report here this discovery and detail our SL modeling.</p><p>The outline of the paper is as follows: in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds2\">2</a> we briefly describe the data and observations used to identify multiple images for the SL analysis, which is presented in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds3\">3</a>. The results are shown and discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds4\">4</a>. We compare our results with those obtained from the Lenstool and GLAFIC pipelines<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn2\">26</a></sup>\n in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds5\">5</a>. Finally, we discuss the previously detected lensed star-forming galaxy in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds6\">6</a> before summarizing our work in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds7\">7</a>. Throughout we assume a \u039bCDM cosmology with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn2.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=4%2FaCaMUN1zsxjKFRpfphGXqdO3w%3D\" alt=\"${{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{m}}0}=0.3$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{m}}0}=0.3</script></span></span>, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn3.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=TFcDHoh%2FkgB%2FfafFVWHfSVGAcZA%3D\" alt=\"${{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{\\Lambda }}0}=0.7$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\rm{\\Omega }}}_{{\\rm{\\Lambda }}0}=0.7</script></span></span>, <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0= 70 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> where 1''\u00a0=\u00a05.337 kpc at the redshift of RXC J0032.1+1808.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds2\" name=\"apjab929ds2\">2.\u00a0Data and Observations</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>RXC0032 is part of the RELICS cluster sample (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>). Each cluster field in the RELICS program was observed for two orbits with Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3/IR) in the F105W, F125W, F140W, and F160W bands, and complemented archival observations with the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS) so that each field is observed for three orbits\u2014one in each of the passbands F435W, F606W, and F814W. In addition, 30 hr per band in each of the Spitzer-IRAC channels (PI: M. Bradac, PI: Soifer) were observed. As one orbit of HST archival observations already existed for RXC0032 (program GO 12166, PI: Ebeling), RELICS observed this galaxy cluster for only two orbits with each of the ACS bands, in addition to the two WFC3 orbits (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>).</p><p>Data reduction of the HST images is described in Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib17\">2019</a>). We used the photometric source catalogs generated with Source Extractor (Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib6\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib6\">1996</a>) in dual-image mode from the final drizzled 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />06 pixel<sup>\u22121</sup> images. The photometric redshifts we use (hereafter <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>), were derived using the Bayesian Photometric Redshift program (BPZ, Ben\u00edtez <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib3\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib3\">2000</a>; Ben\u00edtez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib4\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib4\">2004</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib15\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib15\">2006</a>) from seven HST band imaging data (from the combined RELICS and the aforementioned archival HST data). The reduced imaging, catalogs, and data products are available for the community through the Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes (MAST).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn3\">27</a></sup>\n\n</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds3\" name=\"apjab929ds3\">3.\u00a0Strong-lensing Formalism</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We construct lens models for RXC0032 using two methodologies. Primarily, we use the light-traces-mass (LTM) methodology, outlined in Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>) and references therein; see also Broadhurst et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib10\">2005</a>). With this methodology, we uncover multiple-image sets and publish the first estimate for the size of the lens. We also use a fully parametric formalism for comparison, dubbed hereafter dPIEeNFW. Both methodologies assume two main mass components for the mass distribution: one that accounts for the cluster galaxies, and a second that represents the DM distribution. While member galaxies are also represented differently, the main difference between the two methodologies is that in the LTM methodology the DM distribution is assumed to follow the light distribution, whereas in the parametric model it is independent and follows a combination of symmetric, analytic forms. Both methodologies are implemented on a grid where the resolution can be changed for computational time purposes. Both methodologies are implemented in the same pipeline (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>), and are briefly detailed below.</p><h3 id=\"apjab929ds3-1\" name=\"apjab929ds3-1\">3.1.\u00a0LTM</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The LTM formalism is based on the assumption that the cluster member luminosity-weighted distribution is a reasonable tracer for the DM component in the cluster. The first ingredient for the model are the cluster galaxies, in which each galaxy is assigned a power-law surface density mass distribution, scaled by its luminosity. The exponent is the same for all galaxies and the superposition of all galaxy contributions constitutes the member-galaxies component of the model. This map is then smoothed with a Gaussian kernel to represent the DM distribution component. The two components are then added with a relative weight and scaled to match a multiple-image system (or redshift) of choice. This basic model includes only four free parameters: the power-law exponent for the mass distribution of the cluster galaxies, the width of the smoothing Gaussian, the galaxy to DM weight, and the overall normalization. In addition, we typically include a two-parameter external shear to add further flexibility (manifested mainly in the form of ellipticity of the critical curves), bringing the number of free parameters to six. It is also possible to leave some galaxy masses to be independently scaled\u2014especially for brighter cluster galaxies, for which we typically find the mass to light (<em>M/L</em>) ratio to be a few times higher than that of other cluster members. These key galaxies can also be assigned an ellipticity, and a core. Finally, the redshifts of systems with no spectroscopic redshift can be optimized by the lens model.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab929ds3-2\" name=\"apjab929ds3-2\">3.2.\u00a0dPIEeNFW</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Our parametric formalism is based on analytic functions for both the member galaxies and the DM components. Galaxy-scale halos are parameterized each as a double pseudoisothermal elliptical (dPIE) mass distribution<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn4\">28</a></sup>\n (see, for instance, El\u00edasd\u00f3ttir et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib22\">2007</a>). Their velocity dispersion, core radius and cutoff radius are scaled based on their luminosity following common scaling relations. These are found to describe early-type galaxies well and can be defined with respect to a typical reference luminosity of a galaxy at the cluster's redshift (Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib33\">2007</a>; Monna et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib44\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib44\">2015</a>; Bergamini et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib5\">2019</a>). All cluster galaxies, aside from the few brightest cluster galaxies in the cluster core, have no ellipticities assigned to them and their positional parameters are fixed to those derived from their light distribution. Each large-scale DM halo is represented by an elliptical Navarro, Frenk, and White mass distribution (eNFW, Navarro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib46\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib46\">1996</a>) where their concentration, mass, ellipticity, and position angle are free parameters of the model. The central positions of the eNFW halos can also be freely optimized but here they are fixed to the light centroid of the brightest cluster galaxies.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab929ds3-3\" name=\"apjab929ds3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Minimization</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>For both methodologies, the best-fitting model parameters are found by minimizing the distance in the image plane between the observed and model-predicted positions of the multiple-image centers, via a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> criterion (the equations for the <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> and rms calculations are presented in Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib1\">2019</a>). To do so, we use a Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) engine with a Metropolis\u2013Hastings algorithm that typically includes several thousand steps after the burn-in phase, from which both the best-fit model and the uncertainties are derived. For more details on the modeling scheme, see Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>) and references therein.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab929ds3-4\" name=\"apjab929ds3-4\">3.4.\u00a0SL Analysis of RXC J0032.1+1808</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The starting point of our modeling relies on the construction of a cluster member catalog based on the red-sequence method (Gladders &amp; Yee <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib28\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib28\">2000</a>). We use the magnitudes measured from the F606W and F814W filters to draw a color\u2013magnitude diagram. We only consider galaxies down to 24 AB within \u00b10.3 mag of the sequence (De Lucia &amp; Helmi <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib18\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib18\">2008</a>). To exclude stars from our selection we do not include objects whose magnitudes are brighter than 17 AB or have a stellarity index below &lt;0.95. In addition, we take advantage of the delivered photometric catalog by RELICS to check that all selected cluster members were within <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 of the mean redshift of the cluster (as measured by the BPZ software). We finally perform a visual inspection of the selected cluster members. This allows us to discard further interloping galaxies (bright foreground galaxies for instance) or artifacts (such as faint and diffuse objects or double detections), or add missing galaxies that appear to be cluster members based on their colors (not selected initially due to the strict magnitude cuts applied).</p><p>For the minimization, we consider (for both models) a positional uncertainty of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4. This value has been found to encompass both the underlying statistical uncertainties, the systematic uncertainties between our LTM and dPIEeNFW methods, as well as possible uncertainties arising from structure along the line of sight (Host <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib30\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib30\">2012</a>). To speed up the runtime of the models, and while RELICS HST images have a resolution of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />06 pixel<sup>\u22121</sup>, we adopt a resolution of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />24 pixel<sup>\u22121</sup> for the minimization.</p><p>We present now the multiply imaged systems used in this work, labeled in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a> and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>. We first include the multiply imaged system reported by Dessauges-Zavadsky et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib19\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib19\">2017</a>) who identified six two-knot images belonging to this lensed object. We label this as systems 1 and 2 in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a> and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>, corresponding to the two different emission knots.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab929df1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929df1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=uUf3DfNyE7jJTYiezrll4P%2B6wcY%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=uUf3DfNyE7jJTYiezrll4P%2B6wcY%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Color-composite RGB image of RXC0032. The image was constructed with the HST/ACS passbands F435W (blue), a combination of F606W+F814W (green), and a combination of the HST/WFC3IR passbands F105W+F125W+F140W+F160W (red). The resulting critical curves from our best-fit LTM model are displayed for a source at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.0 (in green) and <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a09.0 (in violet). In addition, we add the critical lines at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a09.0 from our fully parametric best-fit dPIEeNFW model (in red). Multiple images (color coded to ease their identification) are numbered according to Table <a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a>. Cyan systems are reported as candidates and not used in the LTM/dPIEeNFW models. Systems reported by the Lenstool and/or GLAFIC modeling teams are indicated in light gray. Labeled galaxies indicate the cluster members whose weight is freely optimized in the LTM model. The positions of the large-scale dark matter halos for the dPIEeNFW model are fixed to the position of the galaxies A, B, and C. The ellipticities and position angles of the two cluster galaxies, B and C, are freely optimized in both models. Finally, we choose galaxy A as the center for the computation of the convergence profile in Figure <a href=\"#apjab929df2\">2</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab929df1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=uUf3DfNyE7jJTYiezrll4P%2B6wcY%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab929df1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df1_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=O3qWRYwnVrHLeQDmRimlwn0Uo%2BU%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>In addition, based on the full HST ACS + WFC3/IR data set, now including RELICS imaging, we detect additional multiply imaged systems (without a spectroscopic confirmation yet), which we identify based on their morphology and color similarity, as well as predictions from a preliminary LTM model. These images are included as lensing constraints in our final models and we briefly review them here. Systems 3, 4, and c5 straddle the critical curve each forming two bright knots stretching into an arc with their counterimages on the opposite side of the cluster (we note, however, that the identification of the counterimage remains less secure; the LTM, dPIEeNFW, and GLAFIC models implement this image in the SL modeling while the Lenstool model does not). System 6 is lensed into three images appearing as two distinct pink and blue knots (systems 6.1 and 6.2 in our modeling) in the composite HST ACS and WCF3/IR images (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>), making this identification reliable. System 7 is lensed into three images which are identified mainly thanks to their similar, red dropout colors as they do not present any peculiar morphology. This system is a relatively high-redshift dropout object (<em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a04.41 based on its first image for which the photometric redshift is the most reliable), hence its redshift is assumed correct and we fix it in our model. Finally, system 9 is lensed into three diffuse red images (in the ACS and WFC3 composite image). We also identify additional, potential systems that were, however, not included in our SL modeling as constraints. System c8 appears to be lensed into two images straddling the critical curve. We do not, however, identify a third counterimage on the other side of the critical curves so we report it only as a candidate system. System c10 lies next to system 9 and is comprised of two faint emission knots. Our SL models predict additional counterimages near a group of cluster member galaxies located at R.A.\u00a0=\u00a08.0470889 deg; decl.\u00a0= 18.117521 deg. However, due to the light contamination, we cannot make a reliable identification. Systems c11, c12, and c13 consist each of two arcs straddling the critical curve in the northern region of the cluster. Lacking a WFC3/IR coverage in that northern area of the cluster, and BPZ yielding significantly different photometric redshift estimates, we decided to keep these systems as candidate systems. Spectroscopic confirmation of these systems would help to more accurately constrain the mass distribution in the most northern region, where no other lensing constraints are seen.</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><em>LTM</em>. The LTM model is built by considering the weight of the five brightest cluster members (labeled in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>) as free parameters, i.e., allowing their <em>M/L</em> ratio to vary. We also consider as free parameters the ellipticity (varying within a flat, small prior of \u00b10.05 from the measured value from the light distribution) and position angle (varying within \u00b15\u00b0 from the input value we measured) of the bright galaxies located at R.A.\u00a0=\u00a08.04691, decl.\u00a0=\u00a018.118922; and R.A.\u00a0=\u00a08.039185, decl.\u00a0=\u00a018.115616, (galaxies B and C in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>).We scale our model to the spectroscopic redshift of systems 1 and 2 (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a>). The redshift of the remaining systems, except dropout system 7, are left as free parameters to be optimized in the minimization procedure (allowing the relative <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn4.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=cSEMeJMYdmPi4HBg5MBJmGu%2BvTg%3D\" alt=\"${D}_{\\mathrm{LS}}/{D}_{{\\rm{S}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{D}_{\\mathrm{LS}}/{D}_{{\\rm{S}}}</script></span></span> ratio for each system, corresponding to its best-fit <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub> value, to vary by up to \u00b10.05; with <em>D</em><sub>S</sub> and <em>D</em><sub>LS</sub> being the angular diameter distances to the source and between the lens and the source). Taking into account the additional freely optimized cluster members and source redshifts, our final model includes a total of 20 free parameters. The resulting critical curves (for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02 and <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09) for our final best-fit model which has an image reproduction rms\u00a0=\u00a01<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />60, are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>. Finally, the image reproductions by our LTM lens model are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df6\">B1</a>, showing that the overall shape, orientation, and internal details are satisfactorily recovered.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><em>dPIEeNFW</em>. In merging clusters such as the one analyzed here, multiple DM halos are usually incorporated in the modeling in order to better explain the mass distribution. Our best-fit model comprises three large-scale DM halos whose centers are fixed to the position of the cluster members labeled A, B, and C in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>. The large-scale DM halos are parameterized with eNFWs, where their ellipticity parameters, concentration, and mass are optimized. Cluster members are modeled with a dPIE profile with a fixed core radius of 0.2 kpc for the reference galaxy, a velocity dispersion that is allowed to vary between 80\u2013120 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, and a cutoff radius varying from 45\u201365 kpc. They are modeled as spherical with a mag0\u00a0=\u00a0\u221221.56 (a reference magnitude for the scaling relations; Faber &amp; Jackson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib23\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib23\">1976</a>; Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib33\">2007</a>). Similarly as in the LTM model, we leave the ellipticities and position angles of the two bright galaxies presented above to be optimized.We adopt the same multiple images and cluster member catalogs as for the LTM model, leaving as free parameters the redshifts of background sources that have not been spectroscopically confirmed (except for system 7). Our final model includes a total of 24 free parameters. Our best-fit model has an image reproduction of rms\u00a0=\u00a01<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />43 and for comparison, we also show in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a> the resulting critical curves for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds4\" name=\"apjab929ds4\">4.\u00a0Results and Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a> shows the critical curves from our lens models. Both the LTM and dPIEeNFW models, despite having a very different representation for the different mass components, overall yield similar critical curves. This is perhaps somewhat expected, given that similar sets of multiple images were used as constraints, although notable differences exist as well\u2014especially in regions of high magnification or regions with fewer constraints.</p><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df2\">2</a> shows the convergence profile for RXC0032. The SL region is dominated by a large number of substructures, accounting for the shallow inner profile. We also note that the resulting mass distribution has a high overall elongation, or ellipticity, computed as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn5.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=oBrNEJxgXFhaujXWdn7eYnTaubM%3D\" alt=\"$e=({a}^{2}-{b}^{2})/({a}^{2}+{b}^{2})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">e=({a}^{2}-{b}^{2})/({a}^{2}+{b}^{2})</script></span></span>, of <em>e</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a00.74(0.81)\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03 from the LTM(dPIEeNFW) models.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab929df2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929df2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=xpg0L3a0omBZBWPmNvlkS4rMI7A%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=xpg0L3a0omBZBWPmNvlkS4rMI7A%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Convergence profiles from our best-fit LTM and dPIEeNFW models, scaled to a source redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.0. Also plotted for comparison are the Lenstool and GLAFIC models (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab929ds5\">5</a>). All profiles are obtained by radially averaging the convergence map in radial annuli, centered on R.A.\u00a0=\u00a08.049482; decl.\u00a0=\u00a018.143662, indicated with a \"A\" in Figure <a href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a>. The vertical dashed line indicates the radius within which multiple images have been identified.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab929df2\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=xpg0L3a0omBZBWPmNvlkS4rMI7A%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab929df2\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df2_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=B038cH3w%2BX8PVLHBaG0UJgGlBWg%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We compute the value of the effective Einstein radius as <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn6.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=LWUuofsHCdapqMX0ZbqBrt9Dl%2B0%3D\" alt=\"${\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}=\\sqrt{A/\\pi }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}=\\sqrt{A/\\pi }</script></span></span>, with <em>A</em> defined as the area enclosed within the critical curves. Our SL analysis reveals a particularly prominent lens with a resulting Einstein radii of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn7.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=3BBup1NwoDKyIsbLa%2FpBYRY%2BKCs%3D\" alt=\"${\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}({z}_{s}=2)\\,\\sim 38\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 50(39\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 60)\\pm 0\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 20$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}({z}_{s}=2)\\,\\sim 38\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 50(39\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 60)\\pm 0\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 20</script></span></span> and <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>(<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09)\u00a0\u223c\u00a048<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />10(48<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />10)\u00a0\u00b1 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />25 from our LTM(dPIEeNFW) best-fit models, corresponding to an enclosed mass of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dieqn8.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=LGajJ0jqTUJ8IsCmsm32svrLxNU%3D\" alt=\"$M(\\lt {\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}})=2.13(2.67)\\pm 0.2\\,\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">M(\\lt {\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}})=2.13(2.67)\\pm 0.2\\,\\times {10}^{14}{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span> within the <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02 critical curves. The high degree of substructures aggregated in the center yields this very large Einstein radius, similar to only a few other clusters. While the SZ signal, and gas probes in general, lead to the discovery of clusters of high virial mass (Williamson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib73\">2011</a>; Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib52\">2016</a>), a high total mass does not guarantee a large SL region, and in the case of RXC0032, it seems that the high cluster richness, for example, better traces the large Einstein radius. RXC0032 portrays the loose relation\u2014or at least the large scatter in the relation\u2014between gas probes (and the SZ effect in particular) and the central SL area, which depends more closely on various other factors (Giocoli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib27\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib27\">2016</a>) and especially the amount of matter concentrated or projected in the very center, the cluster triaxial shape, and its orientation along the line of sight (King &amp; Corless <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib36\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib36\">2007</a>; Oguri &amp; Blandford <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib50\">2009</a>).</p><p>We show in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df3\">3</a> the <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09 magnification map from our LTM best-fit model as well as the location of the detected high-redshift galaxy candidates within RXC0032's field of view covered by both ACS and WFC3 (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib61\">2020</a>). We compare in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt2\">2</a> the magnification estimates from both methods and provide the intrinsic <em>M</em><sub>uv</sub> at <em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0=\u00a01500 \u00c5 for the high-redshift candidates.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab929df3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929df3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=%2BdunG7btjVEdszJJ22p%2BTj8V064%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=%2BdunG7btjVEdszJJ22p%2BTj8V064%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); The 1<em>\u03c3</em> errors are typically of the size of the star symbol. It should be noted that different clusters have been modeled with different fields of view. type=\"math/tex\">-{18.60}_{-0.90}^{+0.30}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Notes.</strong></p>\n<a name=\"apjab929dt2fna\" id=\"apjab929dt2fna\"></a><sup> a</sup>Galaxy ID, following Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib61\">2020</a>) notations.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt2fnb\" id=\"apjab929dt2fnb\"></a><sup> b</sup>Apparent magnitude in the F125W band.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt2fnc\" id=\"apjab929dt2fnc\"></a><sup> c</sup>Redshift estimation based on the BPZ and EAZY pipelines along with their 1<em>\u03c3</em> uncertainties.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt2fnd\" id=\"apjab929dt2fnd\"></a><sup> d</sup>Best-fit magnification estimates (at the respective source redshift) from the LTM, dPIEeNFW, Lenstool, and GLAFIC models. The statistical uncertainty is computed as the standard deviation from 100 MCMC models. The LTM best-fit value is the one used for all relevant computations.\n<a name=\"apjab929dt2fne\" id=\"apjab929dt2fne\"></a><sup> e</sup>Absolute magnitude, <em>M</em><sub>uv</sub>, at <em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0=\u00a01500 \u00c5 for which the errors have been propagated from the photometric and magnification uncertainties based on our best-fit LTM model. The resulting rest-frame UV luminosities (corrected for lensing magnifications) have a mean of <em>M</em><sub>uv</sub> \u223c\u00a0\u221217.90 with a standard deviation of 1.56.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/898/1/6/suppdata/apjab929dt2_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/ab929d\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dt2_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=zzGBCoWcGJgxvZbHsREfZD8q6pY%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>The derived lensing strength (in the right panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df3\">3</a>) shows that RXC0032 is a very prominent lens, with a large area of high magnification of about \u223c4.4(3.4) arcmin<sup>2</sup> with <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05 to \u223c2.4(1.8) arcmin<sup>2</sup> with <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a010 from our LTM(dPIEeNFW) best-fit models. RXC0032's lensing strength is significantly larger than that of other RELICS clusters modeled with the LTM pipeline (Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib2\">2018</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib1\">2019</a>; Cibirka et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib14\">2018</a>) and similar or higher than those provided by most HFF cluster lenses.</p><p>However, the field of RXC0032 seems to be a unique sight line, compared to similarly-strong cluster lenses. Despite its lensing strength and large critical area, RXC0032 reveals only three <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a06 high-redshift galaxy candidates detected in the field (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib61\">2020</a>); these are characterized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt2\">2</a>. RXJ0152.7-135 (RXJ0152 hereafter; Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib1\">2019</a>) constitutes an interesting counterexample. The strong-lens modeling of both clusters, following the distribution of their member galaxies, reveals two clusters with similar morphologies, i.e., very elongated and showing a high degree of substructure. These effects have been shown to significantly boost the cluster total cross section (Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib41\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib41\">2007</a>). This is clearly evident in the case of RXJ0152, which, despite being a much smaller lens (<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>(<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02)\u00a0\u223c\u00a09''; equivalent to a critical area of 0.06 arcmin<sup>2</sup>), lenses 24 high-redshift galaxy candidates.</p><p>The field of RXC0032 provides the lowest yield of high-redshift candidates in comparison to those listed in the right panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df3\">3</a>. While such a small high-redshift candidate sample can be attributed to cosmic variance (Somerville et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib65\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib65\">2004</a>; Trenti &amp; Stiavelli <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib69\">2008</a>) especially as RELICS targets the brightest distant objects, the low number of high-redshift candidates could also be explained in part by the fact that the HST/WFC3IR's field of view (123''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0137'') is fairly small compared to the size of the lens. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df3\">3</a> (left panel) we show that a significant proportion of high-magnification regions fall outside of the instrument's field of view. Another effect that may play a role in the low number of uncovered high-redshift galaxies is the lower completeness found around the critical curves (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib47\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib47\">2015</a>), and conceivably within them where the intracluster light is brighter. Future deeper observations in a wider field of view around RXC0032 should be able to detect more high-redshift galaxies as well as to examine these hypotheses.</p><p>The high-<em>z</em> galaxy candidate RXC0032+18-0571 (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt2\">2</a>) lies in a very high-magnification area. In the composite (ACS+WFC3) image, this object appears as two distinct light emitting knots, one of which appears to be stretched into an \u223c1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 arc. This is in agreement with our lens model, which predicts a similar stretching for the arc, further supporting the high-<em>z</em> nature of this object. Our SL models also predict a counterimage on the other side of the cluster. However, based on the RELICS high-<em>z</em> photometric study (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib61\">2020</a>), all apparent counterimage candidates have a lower, best-fit photometric redshift estimate. We show the predicted position of the counterimage in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df4\">4</a> based on our LTM model. Our lens model also indicates that the candidates RXC0032+18-0052 and RXC0032+18-0355 could be two images from the same background source, supporting the high-<em>z</em> nature of these objects as well.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab929df4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929df4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=kx3p9p1nSIhUyEM%2Fs7GwoBe%2Fuak%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=kx3p9p1nSIhUyEM%2Fs7GwoBe%2Fuak%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Comparison of the resulting critical curves from the best-fit LTM, dPIEeNFW, Lenstool, and GLAFIC models for a source at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a09.0 on a color-composite image of RXC0032. The yellow and cyan crosses show the best-fit positions of the large-scale DM halos for the Lenstool and GLAFIC models, respectively. The largest discrepancies occur in the regions where no or different constraints are used in the presented models. The magenta stars indicate the location of the high-<em>z</em> galaxy candidates, and the magenta circle shows the possible location of the counterimage for RXC0032+18-0571 based on the redshifts derived by Salmon et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib61\">2020</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab929df4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=kx3p9p1nSIhUyEM%2Fs7GwoBe%2Fuak%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab929df4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df4_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=6eeGKt%2BgU4AH1RKELo7zU3ZmD40%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds5\" name=\"apjab929ds5\">5.\u00a0Comparison</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>As part of the RELICS survey, RXC J0032 has also been modeled with the Lenstool (Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib33\">2007</a>) and GLAFIC (Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib48\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib48\">2010</a>; Kawamata et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib34\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib34\">2016</a>) pipelines whose high-end products are publicly available through the MAST archive<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab929dfn3\">27</a></sup>. In this section, we compare these two models to our modeling results. We give here a short summary of these two models and refer the reader to the references mentioned above for further details.</p><p>It should be noted that often in comparison studies, the same constraints are used throughout with the goal of comparing the different methodologies explicitly (e.g., Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib77\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib77\">2015</a>; Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib43\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib43\">2017</a>). In contrast, here our goal is mainly to probe the credibility of our results, and especially the large Einstein radius estimation. We therefore incorporate the Lenstool and GLAFIC models as well, since these were constructed completely independently by other groups within the RELICS collaboration, including independently identified multiple-image sets (presented in Tables <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt6\">A2</a>). The differences between the results of these different methods also provide the reader with a more quantitative assessment of the magnitude of underlying systematic uncertainties in the presented analysis. It should also be mentioned that while the image recovery rms is often used in assessing the reliability of strong-lens models, a comparison of the rms values of models that use different constraints is of little use (e.g., Johnson &amp; Sharon <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib32\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib32\">2016</a>).</p><p>We first review additional multiple images that were identified independently by the Lenstool (L) and GLAFIC (G) teams and were incorporated in their modeling. System L14 is composed of two multiple images straddling the critical curves in the very northern part of the cluster. However, due to a large rms value for image L14.2, we would consider this a candidate identification. The two multiple images of system L15 appear as orange in the composite image and L15.1 is stretched into an arc and may consist of two merging counterimages. System L16 is lensed into two diffuse arcs, the second being a radial arc. Systems L17 and L18, each composed of three multiple images, appear as brown and pink emission knots in the central region of the cluster, respectively. System L19 comprises three multiple images, each presenting a similar morphology with two emission knots. System L20 lies next to system c8, and is composed of two diffused knots. System G21 is stretched into a blue arc in the composite image with two bright emission knots at each end, and is the only system identified in the southeastern region of this cluster. Finally, system L22 is the most southern multiply imaged system identified and lies next to a small group of cluster members which may introduce a more local galaxy\u2013galaxy lensing effect. To avoid confusion, all multiple images presented throughout this work are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a> and summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a>. Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a> also indicates which images were used as constraints in each model, displayed in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df7\">B2</a>.</p><p>We review the Lenstool and GLAFIC models here.</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><em>Lenstool</em>. This model is built with four large-scale halos parameterized with a dPIE density profile. Their central coordinates, as well as their ellipticity, position angle, core radius, and velocity dispersion, are left to be freely optimized. The best-fit positions for the large-scale DM halos are found to be: R.A., decl.\u00a0=\u00a0(8.048919, 18.1298051); (8.045599, 18.120679); (8.049396, 18.146159); (8.040309, 18.115001), and are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df4\">4</a>. The small-scale halos associated with galaxy members, identified via the red-sequence method, are modeled with a dPIE profile with a fixed core radius of 0.15 kpc, while both the velocity dispersion and the cutoff radius of a fiducial reference galaxy are freely optimized following the adopted scaling relations (Faber &amp; Jackson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib23\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib23\">1976</a>; Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib33\">2007</a>). Aside from systems 1 and 2 that have a spectroscopic redshift measurement, the redshifts of all other multiple images used in the modeling are freely optimized. All multiple images are included in the models with a positional uncertainty of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />3 and the optimization is performed in the image plane. The best-fit model results in an image reproduction of rms\u00a0=\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />59.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><em>GLAFIC</em>. This model includes four eNFW large-scale halos which have been fixed to the following coordinates (displayed in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df4\">4</a>): R.A, decl.\u00a0=\u00a0(8.049478, 18.143654); (8.047251, 18.116557); (8.039177, 18.115615); (8.040402, 18.123657), while the mass, ellipticity, position angle, and concentration parameters are left as free parameters. The SL model also includes cluster members identified with the red-sequence method that are modeled as pseudo-Jaffe ellipsoids (Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib35\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib35\">2001</a>) and following the scaling relations. The redshifts of all multiple images used in the GLAFIC SL model, bar systems 1 and 2, are optimized assuming a Gaussian prior (with <em>\u03b4</em><sub><em>z</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.5) around their photo-<em>z</em> estimates. A positional uncertainty of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />6 is assumed for all multiple images. The GLAFIC best-fit model has an image reproduction of rms\u00a0=\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />49.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p><p>As can be seen (see also Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df1\">1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df4\">4</a>), the parametric models (i.e., dPIEeNFW, Lenstool, and GLAFIC) have each followed a different modeling prescription: the dPIEeNFW has incorporated three large-scale halos; the Lenstool and GLAFIC both have four halos, although their positions slightly vary. The LTM, in contrast, is only semiparametric and adopts a substantially different methodology. In addition, unlike the other parametric models, the dPIEeNFW is constructed on a fixed grid.</p><p>We compare here the main outputs of all four SL models, i.e., the convergence profile, magnification estimates, the resulting critical curves, Einstein radius, and the redshift estimates for the multiple images.</p><p>We show in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df2\">2</a> a comparison of the convergence profiles. All convergence profiles are in good agreement within the 1<em>\u03c3</em> error bars. However, the innermost region of the cluster is less well constrained, possibly due to uncertainties related to the chosen modeling techniques. Another issue is that the statistical uncertainties in the LTM and dPIEeNFW models seem to be smaller than those in the Lenstool and GLAFIC models. We are in the process of examining the origin for this discrepancy, which might be, for instance, related to the finite and lower resolution of the LTM and dPIEeNFW models (which also tends to boost the official <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> quoted for them). In addition, our fixing of system 7, the highest-redshift system used here, to its best photometric redshift value, can also contribute to lowering the errors compared to the two other models.</p><p>The resulting critical curves are found to be in good agreement between all models as shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df4\">4</a>. More significant differences are found in regions with no SL constraints such as the most northern or southeastern regions of the cluster. In the case of the LTM lens model, the differences in the modeling of the southeastern region of the cluster may explain the higher rms value reported for the images 1.5 and 2.5.</p><p>We find that all modeling tools consistently reveal a large Einstein radius (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt3\">3</a>), with a mean and standard deviation estimates of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>(<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.0)\u00a0=\u00a039.44\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.0 and <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>(<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a09.0)\u00a0= 48.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.47.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjab929dt3\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 3.</b>\u00a0\nEffective Einstein Radius for RXC J0032.1+1808\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929dt3\" However, as seen also here, the systematic uncertainty is typically found to be \u223c10% which better reflects the scatter found between different methods.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/898/1/6/suppdata/apjab929dt3_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/ab929d\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929dt3_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=lcCg8%2FiFsyg7LAFfSBJfYNNIJWM%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>All models do also estimate RXC0032 to have a prominent lensing strength and are in very good agreement regarding the total area with high magnification (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df3\">3</a>, right panel). However, as expected, large discrepancies between reconstructions appear around the lens critical lines, or the highest magnification regions (Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib43\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib43\">2017</a>), as is demonstrated by the magnification estimates of the high-<em>z</em> candidates in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt2\">2</a>. These values should thus be used with caution.</p><p>Finally, we compare in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab929df5\">5</a> the model-predicted redshifts among the four models presented in this work, which are also reported in Tables <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt5\">A1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt6\">A2</a>. We find that the LTM and dPIEeNFW models are in good agreement with each other, however they predict systematically higher redshifts than the Lenstool and GLAFIC models. The redshifts obtained with the Lenstool pipeline are slightly overestimated with respect to those derived with GLAFIC but remain in fairly good agreement. Part of the reason for the systematic underestimate of the Lenstool and GLAFIC models compared to the LTM and dPIEeNFW models is that the highest-redshift multiple-image system, system 7, was fixed to its dropout redshift value for the LTM and dPIEeNFW models while in the Lenstool and GLAFIC models it was not, resulting in systematically lower redshifts. It should also be noted that the statistical uncertainties do not encompass the differences between models, which are more representative of the underlying uncertainties.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab929df5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab929df5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=VYyzFATi%2BXMHhIPuImUdzwrjy7Y%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/898/1/6/1/apjab929df5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637170761&amp;Signature=VYyzFATi%2BXMHhIPuImUdzwrjy7Y%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Comparison of the redshift estimates between the different models presented in this work for the multiple images without a spectroscopic redshift confirmation. It will be interesting to revise the differences between the models when more secure redshifts are measured.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab929ds6\" name=\"apjab929ds6\">6.\u00a0The Lensed Star-forming Galaxy at <em>z</em> \u223c 3.6</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The lensed star-forming galaxy at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.6314 (corresponding to our systems 1 and 2) was studied by Dessauges-Zavadsky et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab929dbib19\" id=\"fnref-apjab929dbib19\">2017</a>). They used the Lenstool pipeline to constrain the mass distribution of the RXC0032 lensing model based on the <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.6314 system (considering the two emission knots as in the other models presented in this work), as well as two other, triply imaged systems which were spectroscopically confirmed (although the redshift was not published). They derived the magnification for the lensed images of systems 1 and 2, as shown in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab929dt4\">4</a>, which are
